Litigation Agent Includes Authority for Possession
Ruling on the scope of a litigation agent's authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a litigation agent is also an agent for possession and explain the reasoning based on necessary implications of agency.

Core Idea
A litigation agent automatically has authority to possess the judgment award.

When you appoint someone to handle a lawsuit, do they also have the right to collect what is awarded? The Hanafi school gives a clear answer: yes, the agent for litigation is also an agent for possession. This lesson unpacks that rule and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is appointing an agent for litigation (wukalah fī al-khuṣūmah). The ruling is that this agency automatically includes the power to take possession (qabḍ) of any property or debt awarded by judgment.
The ruling is unanimous among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
2
Understand the reasoning
Agency includes all necessary implications (lawāzim) of the appointed task. Possession is a necessary implication of litigation because without it, the judgment cannot be executed.
This is a general Hanafi principle: the agent's authority covers whatever is indispensable to fulfill the agency.
3
Apply the rule to a concrete scenario
Imagine a creditor appoints a representative to sue a debtor. After winning, the representative can collect the money directly from the debtor without needing a separate power of attorney.
This avoids unnecessary duplication of appointments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[١٤١٢] (٤٣) والوكيل بالخصومة وكيل بالقبض عند ابي حنيفة وابي يوسف ومحمد رحمهم الله تعالى 
اور اس کی تفسير: مقدمے کا وکیل قبضہ کا بھی وکیل ہے امام ابوحنیفہ، امام ابو یوسف اور امام محمد کے نزدیک۔ کسی کو مقدمہ اور خصومت کا وکیل بنایا تو فیصلے کے بعد دین اور چیز پر قبضہ بھی کر سکتا ہے۔ کسی چیز کا وکیل بنایا تو اس کے پورے لوازمات کے ساتھ وکیل ہوگا۔ اور خصومت کے لوازمات میں سے قبضہ کرنا بھی ہے۔
(43) The agent for litigation is an agent for possession according to Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them). Explanation: The agent for a lawsuit and litigation is also agent for possession according to Imam Abu Hanifa, Imam Abu Yusuf, and Imam Muhammad. If someone is appointed as agent for a case and litigation, after the judgment he can take possession of the debt or thing. Because appointing an agent for a thing includes all its necessary implications, and possession is one of the necessary implications of litigation.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage states a rule agreed upon by the three major Hanafi imams: when a person is appointed as an agent for litigation (wukalah fi al-khusumah), the agency automatically includes the authority to take possession (qabd) of any property or debt awarded by the judgment. This avoids the need for a separate agency for execution. The reasoning is that agency includes all necessary accessories (lawazim) of the main task; since taking possession is necessary to complete a successful lawsuit, it is included.

Key Terms
خصومة (khusumah): Litigation, lawsuit, or legal dispute.
قبض (qabd): Taking possession or delivery of an asset or debt obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
An agent is appointed to represent a plaintiff in a debt recovery case. After winning, can the agent collect the money from the debtor?
A) No, he needs separate authorization
B) Yes, he is automatically agent for possession
C) Only if the court orders
D) Only for movable property

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a litigation agent automatically has authority to take possession of the judgment award.

Agent Cannot Buy Specified Item for Himself
Ruling on agent purchasing a specified item

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an agent buying a specified item for himself and explain the rationale of trust.

Core Idea
An agent appointed to buy a specific item commits deception if he buys it for himself; the trust (amanah) forbids it.

This lesson presents a clear prohibition from the Hanafi matn: an agent who is hired to purchase a specific, identified item may not purchase that same item for himself. The ruling is based on the trust (amanah) the principal places in the agent, and buying for oneself constitutes a betrayal akin to deception. Understanding this rule is essential for anyone dealing with agency contracts in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
Read the matn carefully: the act is an agent purchasing a specified item for himself. The ruling is that this is not permissible (haram).
2
Understand the Rationale
The commentary explains that the principal trusted the agent to buy for him. Buying for himself is a type of deception (dhuq). Recognize that the basis is the violation of trust, not merely a breach of contract.
The prohibition is tied to the specific nature of the item; if the item were unspecified, the ruling might differ.
3
Note the Scope of the Ruling
The ruling applies only when the agent is appointed to buy a 'shay' bi'aynihi' (specific thing). If the agency is for any item of a certain type, the agent may buy for himself as long as he doesn't harm the principal.
Memory anchor: 'specified thing' triggers the prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۴۱۰) (۴۱) وان وكله بشراء شيء بعينه فليس له ان يشتريه لنفسه

اگر کسی متعین چیز کے خریدنے کا وکیل بنایا تو اس کے لئے جائز نہیں ہے کہ اس کو اپنے لئے خریدے۔ موکل نے اس پر اعتماد کیا ہے کہ میرے لئے خریدے گا اور وکیل نے اپنے لئے خرید لیا تو اس میں ایک قسم کا دھوکہ دیتا ہوا ۔ اس لئے وکیل متعین چیز کو اپنے لئے نہیں خرید سکتا۔
[1410] (41) And if he appoints him to buy a specific thing, then he cannot buy it for himself. If he was appointed agent to buy a specific thing, then it is not permissible for him to buy it for himself. The principal trusted him to buy for him, and if the agent bought for himself, it is a kind of deception. Therefore, the agent cannot buy the specific thing for himself.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn of the Sameri text. It states a clear prohibition: an agent who is appointed to buy a specific, identified item (e.g., a particular slave or property) may not buy that same item for himself. The underlying reason is that the principal has placed his trust in the agent to acquire the item on his behalf. If the agent buys it for himself, he betrays that trust, which resembles deception. The ruling is based on the principle that the agent must act in the interest of the principal within the scope of the agency. There is no ikhtilaf (dispute) mentioned in this part of the text.

Key Terms
وكل (wakkal): appointed as agent
شيء بعينه (shay' bi'aynihi): a specific, identified thing

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if an agent appointed to buy a specific slave buys that slave for himself?
A) It is permissible if he pays the principal the price.
B) It is not permissible because it breaches trust.
C) It is permissible only if the principal agrees later.
D) It is permissible if the agent buys it at auction.

Key Takeaway
An agent appointed to buy a specified item cannot buy it for himself; doing so violates the trust and constitutes a prohibited deception.

Agent Buying Unspecified Slave: Default Ownership
Ruling on agent buying unspecified slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default ruling on ownership when an agent buys an unspecified slave and identify the exceptions that transfer ownership to the principal.

Core Idea
Agent buys unspecified slave → agent owns it unless intent or principal's money shows otherwise.

When a principal authorises an agent to purchase a slave without specifying which slave, a question arises: who owns the slave the agent buys? The Hanafi ruling gives a clear default based on human custom and legal indications. This lesson unpacks that ruling and its two exceptions.

Guided Path
1
State the default ruling
Identify that when an agent buys an unspecified slave, the default is that the slave belongs to the agent himself, not the principal.
Reason: custom (urf) – people normally buy for themselves.
2
Identify the first exception: express intent
If the agent says 'I intended the purchase for the principal', the slave belongs to the principal.
Intent (niyyah) is a valid indicator when expressed.
3
Identify the second exception: using principal's money
If the agent buys the slave with the principal's money (mal al-muwakkil), the slave belongs to the principal even without verbal statement.
Payment source serves as a clear indication (qarinah).
4
Apply the underlying principle
The rule hinges on whether there is any indication (qarinah) that the purchase was for the principal. Absent such indication, the agent is considered to act for himself.
This principle is derived from general custom and legal maxims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۴۱۱] (۴۲) وان وكله بشراء عبد بغير عينه فاشترى عبدا فهو للوكيل الا ان يقول نويت الشراء للموكل او يشتريه بمال الموكل

ر غیر متعین غلام خریدنے کا وکیل بنایا تھا۔ ایسی صورت میں ایک غلام خریدا تو جب کوئی ایسی علامت نہ ہو کہ یہ موکل کے لئے خریدا ہے اس وقت تک وہ غلام وکیل کے لئے ہی شمار کیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ عموما آدمی اپنے لئے ہی خریدتا ہے۔ موکل کے لئے خریدنے کی علامت میں سے یہ ہے کہ خریدتے وقت موکل کی نیت کرے یا کم از کم موکل کے مال سے غلام خریدے تو وہ غلام موکل کے لئے ہوگا۔
اصول یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ موکل کے لئے خریدنے کی علامت ہو تو موکل کے لئے ہوگا ورنہ اپنی ذات کے لئے ہوگا۔
[1411] (42) And if he appoints him to buy a slave not specified, then he buys a slave, it is for the agent unless he says 'I intended the purchase for the principal' or he buys it with the principal's property. If he was appointed agent to buy an unspecified slave, then in that case, when he buys a slave, as long as there is no indication that it was for the principal, the slave will be considered for the agent. Because generally a person buys for himself. Among the indications of buying for the principal are: that he intends the principal at the time of purchase, or at least buys the slave with the principal's money, then the slave will be for the principal. The principle in this matter is that if there is an indication of buying for the principal, it is for the principal; otherwise, it is for himself.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This is a ruling statement about an agent appointed to buy an unspecified slave. The default rule is that when the agent buys a slave, it belongs to the agent himself, not the principal. This is because there is no evidence that the purchase was made on behalf of the principal. However, there are two exceptions: (1) if the agent expressly states that he intended to buy for the principal, or (2) if he uses the principal's money to make the purchase. In those cases, the slave belongs to the principal. The commentary clarifies that the general custom is that people buy for themselves, so the default is for the agent. The principle is that an indication (qarinah) pointing to the principal is required; otherwise, the purchase is for the agent.

Key Terms
عبد غير معين ('abd ghayr mu'ayyan): an unspecified slave (not identified by particular description)
نوى (nawa): intended (in the heart)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
An agent is appointed to buy an unspecified slave. He buys a slave using his own money without stating his intention. Who owns the slave?
A) The principal, because the agent was appointed to buy.
B) The agent, because there is no indication it was for the principal.
C) The principal, if the slave is suitable for him.
D) The agent, only if the principal rejects the purchase.

Key Takeaway
For an unspecified purchase by an agent, absence of indication means ownership stays with the agent.

Agent for Litigation and Possession: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agency scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi majority position on the authority of a litigation agent to take possession, explain Imam Zufar's objection, and identify the mufta bihi ruling today.

Core Idea
Agency for litigation includes its necessities; but today, fatwa follows Imam Zufar due to widespread dishonesty.

This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute over the scope of an agent's authority. The majority of the school (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad) hold that appointing an agent for litigation implicitly authorizes that agent to take possession of whatever is recovered, as possession is a necessary consequence of the case. However, Imam Zufar disagreed, arguing that such an agent cannot be trusted with actual possession. The commentary reveals that due to the prevalence of betrayal, the operative fatwa today has shifted to Zufar's view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the majority position
State the view of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad: that the agent for litigation is also agent for possession.
This is the Zahir al-Riwayah position.
2
Extract the majority's principle
The reasoning is that agency for something includes all its necessities; possession is a necessity of litigation.
This is a consistent Hanafi principle (al-wakala tashmalu al-lawazim).
3
State the opposing view of Imam Zufar
Imam Zufar argues that a litigation agent is not necessarily a trustworthy person, so allowing possession risks betrayal.
He prioritizes the prevention of harm over the general principle.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Under the majority, if the agent takes possession it is valid; under Zufar, it is invalid and the agent may be liable.
This affects whether the principal must give explicit permission for possession.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position today
Due to widespread dishonesty (khiyana amma), the fatwa today follows Imam Zufar: the litigation agent is not authorized to take possession without explicit permission.
This is an example of a later tarjih based on changing social conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۴۱۲] (۴۳) والوكيل بالخصومة وكيل بالقبض عند ابي حنيفة وابي يوسف و محمد رحمهم الله تعالى

مقدمے کا وکیل قبضہ کا بھی وکیل ہے امام ابوحنیفہ، امام ابو یوسف اور امام محمد کے نزدیک۔
کسی کو مقدمہ اور خصومت کا وکیل بنایا تو فیصلے کے بعد دین اور چیز پر قبضہ بھی کر سکتا ہے۔
کسی چیز کا وکیل بنایا تو اس کے پورے لوازمات کے ساتھ وکیل ہوگا۔ اور خصومت کے لوازمات میں سے قبضہ کرنا بھی ہے۔ اس لئے

قبضہ کرنے کا وکیل ہوگا۔
اس یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ کسی چیز کا وکیل بنا ئیں تو اس کے پورے لوازم کے ساتھ وکیل بنے گا۔ ان امام زفر فر ماتے ہیں کہ خصومت کا وکیل امانت دار نہیں ہوتا اس لئے اگر اس کو دین پر قبضہ کرنے کی گنجائش دیں تو پھر وہ دین موکل کو واپس ہی نہیں دے گا۔ اس لئے خصومت کا وکیل قبضہ کا وکیل نہیں ہوگا۔ اور خیانت عامہ کی وجہ سے آج کل اسی پر فتوی ہے۔
[1412] (43) The agent for litigation is also agent for possession according to Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them).
The agent for litigation is also agent for possession according to Imam Abu Hanifa, Imam Abu Yusuf, and Imam Muhammad.
If someone is appointed agent for a case and litigation, after the decision he can also take possession of the debt and property.
If someone is appointed agent for something, he is agent with all its necessities. And taking possession is among the necessities of litigation. Therefore, he will be agent for taking possession.
This matter is based on the principle that if you appoint an agent for something, he becomes agent with all its necessities. Imam Zufar says that the agent for litigation is not a trustee; therefore, if he is allowed to take possession of the debt, he will not return it to the principal. Therefore, the agent for litigation is not agent for possession. And due to general betrayal, today the fatwa is on this (Imam Zufar's view).
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute. The majority of the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) hold that an agent appointed for litigation (khushumah) is also authorized to take possession (qabd) of the property or debt that is the subject of the case. Their reasoning is based on the principle that appointing an agent for a task includes all necessary ancillary actions; since taking possession is a necessary consequence of a successful litigation, it is included. Imam Zufar differs, arguing that an agent for litigation is not necessarily a trustworthy person, and allowing him to take possession could lead to misappropriation. The commentary notes that due to widespread dishonesty, the fatwa today is according to Imam Zufar's view, meaning that a litigation agent should not be allowed to take possession unless explicitly authorized.

Key Terms
الوكيل بالخصومة (al-wakeel bil-khusoomah): agent appointed to represent the principal in litigation or legal proceedings
القبض (al-qabd): taking physical or constructive possession of property or debt

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Zufar, why is an agent for litigation not authorized to take possession?
A) Because taking possession is not part of litigation.
B) Because the agent is not a trustee and may misappropriate the property.
C) Because the principal must be present for possession.
D) Because the case is not yet finalized.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of agency includes necessary actions, but due to practical considerations, today's fatwa restricts the litigation agent's authority to take possession.

Agent for Debt Collection: Does Litigation Authority Follow?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agent's litigation authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on whether an agent for debt collection automatically has litigation authority, and explain the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
An agent for debt collection may or may not have authority to litigate depending on which Hanafi view is followed.

This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute about the scope of an agent's authority. When a principal appoints someone to collect a debt, does that person also have the power to sue the debtor if necessary? Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, differed on this point. Their disagreement is rooted in different views on whether litigation is a necessary consequence of the task of collection.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that the agent for receipt is also agent for litigation.
Based on the principle that agency for a task includes its necessities.
2
Identify the principle behind Abu Hanifa's view
The principle: appointing an agent for a task implies authorization for all actions necessary to complete that task.
Litigation may be necessary to collect a debt.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the agent for receipt is not agent for litigation.
4
Identify the principle behind the Sahibayn's view
Receipt and litigation are distinct tasks requiring different skills and trust.
Trust in one does not imply trust in the other.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If the agent files a lawsuit without explicit authorization, is it valid? According to Abu Hanifa, yes; according to the Sahibayn, no, and the principal may repudiate it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۴۱۳] (۴۴) والوكيل بقبض الدين وكيل بالخصومة فيه عند ابي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالی

قرض پر قبضہ کا وکیل مقدمے کا بھی وکیل ہوگا امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک۔ وجہ یہ ہے کہ قرض پر قبضہ کرنے کے لئے بعض مرتبہ مقدمہ بھی کرنا پڑتا ہے۔ اور پہلے قاعدہ گزر چکا ہے کہ کسی کام کا وکیل بنائیں تو اس کے پورے لوازمات کے ساتھ وکیل بنانا پڑتا ہے۔ اس لئے قرض کا وکیل مقدمہ اور خصومت کا بھی وکیل ہوگا۔
نا صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ قرض پر قبضہ کا وکیل مقدمے کا وکیل نہیں ہوگا۔ دین پر قبضہ کرنا اور چیز ہے جو امانت دار کا کام ہے اور مقدمہ کرنا اور چیز ہے جو چالاک اور ماہرین قانون کا کام ہے۔ اس لئے دونوں دو الگ الگ کام ہیں۔ اور کوئی ضروری نہیں ہے کہ جو امانت دار ہو وہ قانون کا ماہر بھی ہو۔ اور اس پر قانونی اعتماد بھی کیا جائے۔ اس لئے قبضہ کا وکیل خصومت اور مقدمہ کا وکیل نہیں ہو گا۔
اصول یہ اس اصول پر گئے ہیں کہ قبضہ اور خصومت دو الگ الگ کام ہیں۔ ایک پر اعتماد کرنے سے دوسرے کام میں اعتماد کرنالازم نہیں آتا۔
[1413] (44) The agent for receiving the debt is agent for litigation in it according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him).
The agent for receiving a debt will also be agent for litigation according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The reason is that sometimes litigation is necessary to receive the debt. And the earlier principle has passed that if you appoint an agent for a task, he is appointed with all its necessities. Therefore, the agent for debt will also be agent for litigation.
The two (Sahibayn: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say that the agent for receiving a debt is not agent for litigation. Receiving the debt is one thing, which is the work of a trustee, and litigation is another thing, which is the work of a clever person and legal expert. Therefore, they are two separate tasks. It is not necessary that a trustee be a legal expert and be legally trusted. Therefore, the agent for receipt is not agent for litigation. They based this on the principle that receipt and litigation are two separate tasks. Trust in one does not entail trust in the other.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage presents another intra-Hanafi dispute. Abu Hanifa holds that an agent appointed specifically to receive a debt (qabd al-dayn) is automatically also an agent for litigation if needed to collect that debt. His reasoning is based on the principle of 'necessity'—since collecting a debt may require a court case, appointing an agent for receipt implicitly authorizes necessary legal action. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, arguing that receiving a debt is a trust-based task (amanah), while litigation requires legal expertise and a different kind of trust. They maintain that appointing someone as a collection agent does not make him a litigation agent, as the two roles are distinct and require different skills and responsibilities.

Key Terms
قبض الدين (qabd al-dayn): receiving payment of a debt
الخصومة (al-khusoomah): litigation, legal proceedings

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say an agent for receiving a debt is not an agent for litigation?
A) Because litigation is not necessary for all debts.
B) Because receiving a debt is a trust-based task while litigation requires legal expertise.
C) Because the principal must personally file the lawsuit.
D) Because the agent might misuse the court process.

Key Takeaway
An agent for debt collection does not automatically have litigation authority according to the Sahibayn's view, which is often relied upon in later Hanafi practice.

8 min
Acknowledgment by Litigation Agent: Court vs. Outside
Ruling on validity of agent's acknowledgment against principal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a litigation agent's acknowledgment binds the principal and distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad from Imam Zufar.

Core Idea
An agent's acknowledgment binds the principal only if made before the judge; outside court it has no effect.

This passage addresses a critical nuance in agency law: can a court-appointed agent confess on behalf of his client? The Hanafi school distinguishes sharply between the courtroom and elsewhere. Understanding this distinction protects the principal from unauthorized admissions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the agent's acknowledgment (iqrar) against the principal. The ruling: valid if before the judge, invalid if elsewhere.
The text explicitly states 'jaza iqraruhu' (valid) before judge and 'la yajuz' (invalid) outside.
2
Locate the dalil
The ruling is based on the nature of agency for litigation: absolute authority includes all necessary responses.
The proof is implicit; the commentary expands on the reasoning.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The legal link: agency for litigation (wakala fi al-khusuma) authorizes the agent to do whatever is needed in court, including acknowledgment, because it is a possible response.
This view is held by Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam Zufar (and others) argue that acknowledgment contradicts the purpose of litigation (denial/dispute), so it is never valid.
Zufar's view restricts the agent's role to defense only.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi school relies on the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: valid in court. This is the mufta bihi (operative position).
The commentary emphasizes that absolute agency covers all methods.

[۱۴۱۴] (۴۵) واذا اقر الوكيل بالخصومة على موكله عند القاضی جاز اقراره ولا يجوز اقراره عليه عند غير القاضی

اگر مقدمے کا وکیل اپنے موکل پر اقرار کرے قاضی کے پاس تو اس کا اقرار جائز ہے اور وکیل کا اقرار قاضی کے علاوہ کے پاس جائز نہیں ہے امام ابو حنیفہ اور امام محمد کے نزدیک مگر یہ کہ وہ مقدمہ سے نکل جائے گا۔
ایک آدمی کو وکیل بنایا کہ میری جانب سے قاضی کے سامنے خصومت اور مقدمے کے وکیل بنیں تو اس کے دو مطلب ہیں۔ ایک تو یہ کہ آپ اس بات کے وکیل ہیں کہ آپ موکل کے جرم کا اقرار نہ کریں بلکہ ہمیشہ انکار ہی کرتے چلے جائیں یا زیادہ سے زیادہ خاموش رہیں۔اسی لئے آپ کو خصومت کا وکیل مقرر کیا ہے۔ اگر اقرار کرنا ہوتا تو میں خود اقرار کر لیتا آپ کو وکیل خصومت بنانے کی ضرورت کیا تھی؟ یہی مطلب امام زفر اور ائمہ ثلاثلہ لیتے ہیں۔ وہ فرماتے ہیں کہ خصومت کا مطلب ہی جھگڑا کرنا اور انکار کرنا ہے، اور اقرار کرنا اس کی ضد ہے۔ اس لئے وکیل ضد کا مالک کیسے بنے گا؟ اس لئے وکیل یا انکار کرے یا دیکھے کہ میرا موکل واقعی مجرم ہے تو خاموش رہے۔ البتہ اقرار نہ کرے۔ دوسرا مطلب یہ ہے کہ وہ مطلق جواب کا وکیل ہے جس میں اقرار بھی شامل ہے اور انکار بھی شامل ہے اور خاموش بھی رہ سکتا ہے۔ وہ متینوں طریقوں کا مالک ہے۔ کیونکہ مطلق خصومت میں تینوں طریقے شامل ہیں۔ البتہ چونکہ خصومت کا وکیل ہے اور خصومت ہوتی ہے قاضی کی مجلس
[1414] (45) And if the agent for litigation acknowledges against his principal before the judge, his acknowledgment is valid; and his acknowledgment against him before other than the judge is not valid.
If the agent for litigation acknowledges against his principal before the judge, it is valid; and the agent's acknowledgment before other than the judge is not valid according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, unless he withdraws from the case.
If a person appoints an agent to be his representative in litigation before the judge, there are two interpretations. One: that you are the agent not to confess the principal's guilt but to always deny or at most remain silent. For this reason, he appointed you as agent. If confession was intended, he would have confessed himself. This is the meaning taken by Imam Zufar and the three Imams. They say that the meaning of litigation is to dispute and deny, and confession is its opposite. Therefore, how can the agent be the owner of the opposite? Therefore, the agent should either deny or if he sees that his principal is truly guilty, remain silent. He should not confess. The second meaning: that he is the agent for absolute response, which includes both confession and denial, and can also remain silent. He is the master of these methods. Because absolute litigation includes all three methods. However, since he is the agent for litigation, and litigation takes place in the court of the judge.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific ruling about the authority of an agent for litigation to make an acknowledgment (iqrar) against his principal. The ruling states that such an acknowledgment is valid only if made before a judge (qadi), not outside the court. The commentary presents two interpretations. The first, held by Imam Zufar and the 'three imams' (possibly the other major Sunni schools), argues that the agent is appointed only to defend, not to confess; therefore, he cannot make an acknowledgment. The second interpretation, which is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, holds that the agent is authorized for all forms of response in litigation, including acknowledgment, as long as it is done in court. The logic is that absolute agency for litigation covers all necessary actions, and the court setting ensures proper procedure. The commentary notes that acknowledgment outside court is not valid because the agent's authority is limited to the courtroom.

Key Terms
أقر (aqarra): acknowledged, confessed (legally binding statement against oneself)
عند القاضي ('inda al-qadi): before the judge (in the formal court setting)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Anywhere, as long as it is in writing.
B) Only before a judge (in court).
C) Only if the principal is present.
D) Only if the agent also denies the claim.

Key Takeaway
An agent's acknowledgment in litigation binds the principal only if made before the judge; outside court it is invalid.

Agent for Debt Collection and Litigation: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Scope of Agency
Ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on scope of agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whether an agent for debt collection is also an agent for litigation, and apply the principle of necessary implications (lawazim) to similar agency scenarios.

Core Idea
Appointment to a task includes its necessary implications, but changing circumstances can shift the operative fatwa.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the scope of agency when a principal appoints an agent to collect a debt. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the agent automatically also has authority to litigate, based on the principle that appointment to a task includes its necessary implications (lawazim). The Sahibayn and Imam Zufar distinguish between collection and litigation as separate skills, arguing that one does not imply the other. The commentary notes that due to widespread betrayal (khiyanah 'ammah), the fatwa later shifted to Zufar's view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
The core question: if someone is appointed as agent to collect a debt, is he also appointed as agent to litigate for that debt?
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and its basis
Abu Hanifa says yes, because collection may require litigation, and appointing for a task includes its necessary implications (lawazim).
3
State the opposing positions
Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and Imam Zufar say no: collection is a matter of trust, litigation requires legal expertise; they are separate tasks.
4
Analyze the concept of khiyanah 'ammah
Understand how widespread dishonesty affected the fatwa, leading to adoption of Zufar's view in practice.
5
Apply the principle to a new scenario
Given a novel agency situation, determine scope using the lawazim principle and consider whether khiyanah 'ammah would trigger a shift.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يوسف و محمد رحمهم الله تعالى [۱۴۱۳ (۴۴) والوكيل بقبض الدين وكيل بالخصومة فيه عند ابي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالی قبضہ کرنے کا وکیل ہوگا۔ اس یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ کسی چیز کا وکیل بنا ئیں تو اس کے پورے لوازم کے ساتھ وکیل بنے گا۔ ان امام زفر فر ماتے ہیں کہ خصومت کا وکیل امانت دار نہیں ہوتا اس لئے اگر اس کو دین پر قبضہ کرنے کی گنجائش دیں تو پھر وہ دین موکل کو واپس ہی نہیں دے گا۔ اس لئے خصومت کا وکیل قبضہ کا وکیل نہیں ہوگا۔ اور خیانت عامہ کی وجہ سے آج کل اسی پر فتوی ہے۔ ۱۴۱۳] (۴۴) قرض پر قبضہ کا وکیل مقدمے کا بھی وکیل ہوگا امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک۔ وجہ یہ ہے کہ قرض پر قبضہ کرنے کے لئے بعض مرتبہ مقدمہ بھی کرنا پڑتا ہے۔ اور پہلے قاعدہ گزر چکا ہے کہ کسی کام کا وکیل بنائیں تو اس کے پورے لوازمات کے ساتھ وکیل بنانا پڑتا ہے۔ اس لئے قرض کا وکیل مقدمہ اور خصومت کا بھی وکیل ہوگا۔ نا صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ قرض پر قبضہ کا وکیل مقدمے کا وکیل نہیں ہوگا۔ دین پر قبضہ کرنا اور چیز ہے جو امانت دار کا کام ہے اور مقدمہ کرنا اور چیز ہے جو چالاک اور ماہرین قانون کا کام ہے۔ اس لئے دونوں دو الگ الگ کام ہیں۔ اور کوئی ضروری نہیں ہے کہ جو امانت دار ہو وہ قانون کا ماہر بھی ہو۔ اور اس پر قانونی اعتماد بھی کیا جائے۔ اس لئے قبضہ کا وکیل خصومت اور مقدمہ کا وکیل نہیں ہو گا۔ اصول یہ اس اصول پر گئے ہیں کہ قبضہ اور خصومت دو الگ الگ کام ہیں۔ ایک پر اعتماد کرنے سے دوسرے کام میں اعتماد کرنالازم نہیں آتا۔
Yusuf and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them. (1413) (44) And the agent for collecting a debt is an agent for litigation regarding it according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. He will be an agent for collecting. This ruling is based on the principle that if one appoints an agent for something, that agent is appointed with all its necessary implications. Imam Zufar says: The agent for litigation is not a trustworthy person, so if we allow him to collect the debt, he will not return it to the principal. Therefore, the agent for litigation is not an agent for collection. Due to widespread betrayal, today the fatwa is given accordingly. (1413) (44) The agent for collecting a loan is also an agent for litigation according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The reason is that to collect a loan, sometimes litigation is required. And the earlier principle has been mentioned: if one appoints an agent for a task, he is appointed with all its necessary implications. Therefore, the agent for the loan is also an agent for litigation and dispute. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: The agent for collecting a loan is not an agent for litigation. Collecting a debt is one thing, which is the work of a trustworthy person, and litigating is another thing, which is the work of clever legal experts. So they are two separate tasks. It is not necessary that one who is trustworthy is also a legal expert, and that legal confidence is placed in him. Therefore, the agent for collection is not an agent for litigation and dispute. They base this on the principle that collection and litigation are two separate matters; trust in one does not necessitate trust in the other.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute. The matn states the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa: an agent appointed to collect a debt is also authorized to litigate for it. The commentary explains the reasoning and the opposing views. Abu Hanifa applies a general principle: appointment to a task includes what is necessary for its completion. Since litigation may be needed to collect a debt, the agent for collection is also agent for litigation. Imam Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree. Zufar argues that a litigation agent is not trustworthy enough for collection; the Sahibayn argue that collection and litigation are distinct skills. The commentary notes that due to widespread betrayal (khiyanah 'ammah), the fatwa today (in the author's time) follows Zufar's view, limiting the agent's authority to litigation only. This illustrates how later Hanafi practice may shift due to changing circumstances.

Key Terms
وکيل بقبض الدين (wakīl bi-qabḍ al-dayn): An agent appointed specifically to collect a debt owed to the principal.
خصومة (khuṣūmah): Litigation or legal dispute before a judge.
خيانة عامة (khiyānah ʿāmmah): Widespread betrayal or dishonesty among people, affecting the trustworthiness of agents.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because collection is impossible without litigation
B) Because appointment to a task includes its necessary implications
C) Because the agent is always trustworthy for both tasks
D) Because the Sahibayn also agree with this ruling

Key Takeaway
The scope of agency is determined by necessary implications of the task, but later Hanafi fatwa may shift due to prevalent dishonesty.

Abu Yusuf: Agent's Iqrar Valid Outside Court
Abu Yusuf's ruling on agent's iqrar outside court

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the agent's iqrar outside court and explain the qiyas reasoning used by Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
An agent's acknowledgment binds the principal even outside court—the agent stands in the principal's place.

In Hanafi fiqh, the validity of an agent's iqrar (acknowledgment) depends on the scope of agency. This passage from Imam Abu Yusuf addresses a specific scenario: whether the agent's acknowledgment made before someone other than a judge is binding on the principal. Abu Yusuf rules affirmatively, drawing an analogy to the principal's own iqrar. Understanding this ruling clarifies the agency relationship and the limits of the agent's power to bind the principal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the agent's iqrar (acknowledgment) against the principal. The ruling, according to Abu Yusuf, is that it is valid (jāʾiz) even when made outside a judge's court.
2
Locate the dalil – the qiyas reasoning
Abu Yusuf uses qiyas: the agent is compared to the principal. The principal's iqrar is valid anywhere, so by analogy, the agent's iqrar should also be valid anywhere, provided the agent is acting within his authority.
This is an example of qiyas jaliy (clear analogy).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah – the legal link
The effective cause ('illa) is that the agent stands in the place of the principal (wakil bi manzilat al-muwakkil). Since the principal's iqrar is not restricted to a judge's presence, the agent's iqrar is likewise unrestricted.
The 'illa is the agency relationship itself, which transmits the principal's capacity to the agent.
4
Determine the practical implication
This ruling expands the binding effect of the agent's iqrar. It means that the principal cannot later deny an iqrar made by his authorized agent outside court.
This protects third parties who rely on the agent's statements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و قال ابو يوسف رحمه الله تعالى يجوز اقراره عليه عند غير القاضی [امام ابو یوسف نے فرمایا جائز ہے وکیل کا اقرار کر نا موکل پر قاضی کے علاوہ کے پاس بھی۔ وکیل نے قاضی کی مجلس کے علاوہ میں موکل پر اقرار کر لیا تب بھی اقرار ہو جائے گا امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک ۔ وہ فرماتے ہیں کہ وکیل موکل کے درجے میں ہے اور موکل قاضی کی مجلس کے علاوہ میں کسی چیز کا اقرار کرے تو اقرار ہو جاتا ہے تو وکیل بھی قاضی کی مجلس کے علاوہ میں اقرار کرے تو اقرار ہو جائے گا۔]
And Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, said: His acknowledgment against him is valid when before other than the judge. [Imam Abu Yusuf said: It is permissible for the agent to acknowledge against the principal also in front of someone other than the judge. If the agent acknowledged against the principal outside the judge's court, then the acknowledgment still takes effect according to Imam Abu Yusuf. He says that the agent is in the position of the principal, and if the principal makes an acknowledgment outside the judge's court, the acknowledgment takes effect; so too if the agent makes an acknowledgment outside the judge's court, it takes effect.]
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from Imam Abu Yusuf within the Hanafi school. The issue is whether an agent's (wakil) acknowledgment (iqrar) against his principal (muwakkil) is valid if made before someone other than a judge (qadi). Imam Abu Yusuf rules that it is valid. His reasoning is based on qiyas (analogical reasoning): the agent stands in the place of the principal (wakil bi manzilat al-muwakkil). Since the principal's own acknowledgment is valid outside the judge's court, the agent's acknowledgment is likewise valid. This ruling is an expansion of the general principle of agency in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
اقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment or confession of a right or obligation.
وکیل (wakil): Agent or representative appointed by the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only in front of a judge
B) Only in front of two witnesses
C) Also outside the judge's court
D) Only in the principal's presence

Key Takeaway
An agent's iqrar binds the principal even outside court because the agent stands in the principal's place, as established by qiyas.

Claim of Agency to Collect Debt of Absent Person
Ruling on claim of agency to collect debt from debtor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sequential rulings when a person claims agency to collect a debt of an absent principal and apply the consequences of confirmation or denial.

Core Idea
The debtor's payment to a claimed agent is valid only if the absent principal later confirms the agency; otherwise the debtor must pay again.

This ruling from the Hanafi fiqh text addresses a common situation: someone claims to be the agent of an absent creditor to collect a debt. It outlines the debtor's obligations and the effects of the absent principal's confirmation or denial. Understanding this rule protects the debtor from double payment and clarifies the agent's liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the claim
Recognize the three parties: the claimant (alleged agent), the absent principal (creditor), and the debtor. The claimant asserts he is authorized to collect the debtor's debt on behalf of the absent principal.
The claim is unilateral; no formal proof of agency is required at this stage.
2
Understand the debtor's confirmation and its immediate effect
When the debtor confirms the claimant's agency, the debtor is ordered to deliver the debt to the claimant. This step transfers possession to the claimant but does not yet discharge the debt.
The debtor's confirmation is based on his own risk; he accepts the claimant as agent using his own property.
3
Distinguish outcomes after the principal appears
If the absent principal later confirms the agency, the payment becomes final and the debt is discharged. If the principal denies agency, the payment is invalid and the debtor must pay the principal again.
The principal's denial retroactively invalidates the debtor's payment.
4
Recognize the debtor's recourse against the agent
If the principal denies agency and the money is still in the agent's hand, the debtor can recover it from the agent. If the money is lost, the debtor bears the loss because the money was held as a trust (amanah) by the agent.
The agent's liability is limited to the extent of actual possession; loss without negligence is not recoverable.
5
Note the underlying principle
The ruling is based on the principle that a person may accept another as agent over his own property at his own risk. The debtor's confirmation does not bind the absent principal.
This protects the absent principal's rights while providing limited recourse to the debtor.

Source Text

ومن ادعى انه وكيـل الـغـائب في قبض دينه فصدقه الغريم امر بتسليم الدين اليه فان حضر الغائب فصدقه جاز والا دفع اليه الغريم الدين ثانيا ويرجع به على الوكيل ان كان باقيا في يده [کسی نے دعوی کیا کہ وہ غائب کا وکیل ہے اس کے قرض کے قبضہ کرنے میں، پس مقروض نے اس کی تصدیق کر دی تو مقروض کو حکم دیا جائے گا قرض سپرد کرنے کا، پس اگر غائب حاضر ہو گیا اور اس نے وکیل کی تصدیق کر دی تو جائز ہو گیا ورنہ تو مقروض موکل کی طرف دین دوبارہ ادا کرے گا اور دین لے گا وکیل سے اگر اس کے ہاتھ میں باقی ہو۔ النترا مثلا زید نے دعوی کیا کہ وہ عمر کا وکیل ہے اس بات کا کہ اس نے کہا ہے کہ خالد سے قرض وصول کر لو۔ اور خالد مقروض نے تصدیق کر دی کہ واقعی تم عمر کے وکیل ہو تو خالد نے چونکہ تصدیق کر دی کہ زید کا عمر وکیل ہے اور مال خالد کا ذاتی ہے ، وہ اپنے مال میں تصرف کر سکتا ہے اس لئے خالد کو حکم دیا جائے گا کہ عمر کا قرض زید کے حوالے کر دے۔ پھر عمر باہر سے واپس آیا اور تصدیق کر دی کہ زید میرا وکیل ہے تو بات بن گئی اور خالد کا ادا کیا ہوا قرض عمر کو ادا ہو گیا۔ اور اگر عمر موکل نے کہا کہ زید میر اوکیل نہیں ہے تو خالد کو کہا جائے گا کہ تم دوبارہ عمر کا قرض عمر کو ادا کرو۔ کیونکہ عمر نے خالد کو با ضابطہ نہیں کہا تھا کہ زید میرے دین پر قبضہ کرنے کا وکیل ہے بلکہ یہ تو زید اور خالد کی ملی بھگت تھی کہ خالد نے تصدیق کر دی کہ تم عمر کے وکیل ہو۔ اس لئے خالد کو دوبارہ قرض عمر کی طرف ادا کرنا ہو گا۔ اور زید کے ہاتھ میں دی ہوئی رقم موجود ہو تو اس سے خالد واپس لے گا اور وہ رقم ہلاک ہو گئی تو اس سے قانونی طور پر واپس نہیں لے سکے گا۔ وہ مال زید کے ہاتھ میں امانت کا تھا اس لئے اس کے ہاتھ میں ہلاک ہو جائے تو واپس نہیں لے سکے گا۔ انہوں یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ اپنے مال میں کسی کو وکیل تسلیم کر سکتا ہے اور اس کو اپنا مال حوالے کر سکتا ہے۔]
And whoever claims that he is the agent of an absent person for collecting his debt, and the debtor confirms him, he is ordered to deliver the debt to him. Then if the absent person comes and confirms him, it is valid; otherwise, the debtor pays the debt to him a second time and recovers it from the agent if it remains in his hand. [Someone claimed that he is the agent of an absent person for collecting his debt; then the debtor confirmed him, so the debtor is ordered to hand over the debt. Then if the absent person comes and confirms the agent, it becomes valid; otherwise, the debtor will pay the debt again to the principal and will take back the debt from the agent if it remains in his hand. For example, Zaid claimed that he is the agent of Umar for collecting debt from Khalid, and Khalid the debtor confirmed that Zaid is Umar's agent. Since Khalid confirmed it and the money is Khalid's own, he can dispose of his property, so Khalid is ordered to hand over Umar's debt to Zaid. Then Umar returned and confirmed that Zaid is his agent, so the matter is settled and Khalid's payment of the debt is considered paid to Umar. But if Umar said that Zaid is not his agent, then Khalid will be told to pay Umar's debt again to Umar, because Umar had not formally told Khalid that Zaid is his agent to collect the debt; rather it was collusion between Zaid and Khalid that Khalid confirmed that Zaid is Umar's agent. Therefore, Khalid must repay the debt to Umar. And if the money given to Zaid is still in his possession, Khalid can recover it from him; if it is destroyed, he cannot legally recover it, because that money was a trust in Zaid's hand, so if it is destroyed in his hand, he cannot recover it. The basis of this issue is that a person can accept someone as an agent over his own property and can hand over his property to him.]
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling about a person who claims to be the agent of an absent principal to collect a debt from a debtor. The ruling proceeds in stages: First, if the debtor confirms the claimant's agency, the debtor is ordered to hand over the debt to the claimant. Second, if the absent principal later appears and confirms the agency, the payment is valid and discharges the debtor's obligation. Third, if the principal denies the agency, the debtor must pay the debt again to the principal. The debtor can then recover the amount from the agent if the money is still in the agent's possession; if the money is lost, the debtor bears the loss because it was a trust (amanah) in the agent's hand. The underlying principle is that a person may accept another as agent over his own property at his own risk, but the debtor's confirmation alone does not bind the absent principal.

Key Terms
وكيل الغائب (wakil al-gha'ib): Agent of an absent person, specifically for collecting debts.
غريم (gharim): Debtor, the one who owes the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a claimant says he is the agent of an absent person to collect a debt, and the debtor pays him, but later the absent person denies the agency, what must the debtor do?
A) He is free from debt
B) He must pay the debt again to the absent person
C) He must sue the claimant
D) He can keep the money

Key Takeaway
The debtor's payment to an alleged agent is provisional; it becomes final only if the absent principal confirms the agency.

Claim of Agency to Take Deposit Not Enforceable
Ruling on claim of agency to take possession of a deposit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the claim of agency to take a deposit and distinguish it from the case of debt collection.

Core Idea
A depositor cannot hand over another's deposit based on a claimant's word alone, even if the depositor confirms the agency.

Imagine someone comes to you and says, 'I am the agent of the owner, give me the deposit.' Even if you believe him and confirm his agency, Hanafi law does not compel you to hand over the deposit. Why? Because the deposit belongs to the absent owner, and you are merely a trustee. This lesson explains the ruling and the principle behind it, and how it differs from the case of debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is a claim of agency to take a deposit. The ruling: the depositor is not ordered to hand over the deposit, even if the depositor confirms the claim.
Focus on the depositor's confirmation not creating an obligation.
2
Compare with the debt case
In debt, the debtor pays from his own property, so his confirmation orders payment. In deposit, the property is not the depositor's, so no such order.
The key difference is ownership of the property.
3
Identify the underlying principle
One cannot hand over another's property without the owner's explicit authorization. The depositor's confirmation does not constitute such authorization.
This principle protects the absent owner's rights.
4
Apply the condition
The condition for delivery is proof of the owner's authorization (e.g., a clear statement from the owner, not just the depositor's confirmation).
Without this condition, the depositor must refuse.
5
Note the consequence
If the depositor hands over wrongly, he becomes liable (daman) for the deposit to the owner.
The depositor acts at his own risk if he chooses to deliver voluntarily.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان قال انى وكيل بقبض الوديعة فصدقه المودع لم يؤمر بالتسليم اليه [اور اگر کہا کہ میں امانت کے قبضہ کرنے کا وکیل ہوں اور امانت رکھنے والے نے اس کی تصدیق کر دی تو اس کو حوالہ کرنے کا حکم نہیں دیا جائے گا۔ امانت کی چیز میں وہی چیز دی جاتی ہے جو امانت رکھی گئی ہے۔ امانت رکھنے والا اپنی طرف سے کوئی چیز نہیں دے سکے گا۔ اس لئے خالد نے تصدیق کردی کہ زید وکیل ہے تو عمر کی امانت شدہ چیز زید کے حوالے کرنے کے لئے نہیں کہا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ عمر نے نہیں کہا ہے کہ زید میرا وکیل ہے۔ وہ اب تک غائب ہے اس لئے امانت کی چیز وکیل کے حوالے کرنے نہیں کہا جائے گا۔ اوپر کے مسئلے میں تو قرض کی رقم خود خالد کی رقم تھی اس لئے دینے کو کہا گیا، یہاں امانت کی رقم عمر کی ہے خالد کی نہیں ہے۔ اصول یہ مسئلہ اس پر ہے کہ دوسرے کی چیز وکالت کی تصدیق کے باوجود حوالے کرنے نہیں کہا جائے گا۔ مودع : امانت پر رکھنے والا آدمی ، ودع سے مشتق ہے۔]
And if he says, 'I am an agent to take possession of the deposit,' and the depositor confirms him, he is not ordered to deliver it to him. [And if he says, I am an agent to take possession of the trust, and the trustee confirms him, then he will not be ordered to hand it over to him. For the trust property, the exact thing that was deposited is to be given. The trustee cannot give something from himself. Therefore, if Khalid confirmed that Zaid is an agent, he will not be ordered to hand over Umar's deposited property to Zaid, because Umar did not say that Zaid is his agent. He is still absent, so the deposited property will not be ordered to be handed over to the agent. In the previous issue, the debt amount was Khalid's own money, so he was ordered to give it; here the deposited property is Umar's, not Khalid's. The principle of this issue is that one will not be ordered to hand over another's property even if the agency is confirmed. Mudi' (depositor): the person who deposits, derived from wadi'a.]
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between a claim of agency to collect a debt and a claim of agency to take a deposit. In the debt case, the debtor pays from his own property, so his confirmation of the agent's claim is sufficient to authorize the payment. In the deposit case, the depositor holds another's property as a trust; he cannot hand it over based solely on a claimant's assertion and the depositor's own confirmation, because the property belongs to the absent owner. The depositor is not the owner; he is merely a trustee. Therefore, the court does not order delivery. The underlying principle: one cannot hand over another's property merely on the basis of an alleged agency confirmed by the holder; the owner's explicit authorization is required.

Key Terms
وديعة (wadi'a): Deposit; property entrusted to someone for safekeeping.
مودع (mudi'): Depositor; the person who gives the deposit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, if a person claims to be the agent of an absent owner to take a deposit, and the depositor confirms him, is the depositor ordered to hand over the deposit?
A) Yes, because the depositor confirmed
B) No, because the deposit belongs to the absent owner
C) Yes, but only if the deposit is money
D) No, unless the absent owner is present

Key Takeaway
A depositor is not ordered to hand over a deposit to a claimant of agency solely on the basis of the depositor's confirmation; the owner's explicit authorization is required.

Handing Trust to an Unauthorized Agent: The Depositary's Duty
Explains when a depositary is not ordered to deliver trust to an agent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a depositary is not ordered to deliver a trust to an agent without the owner's explicit authorization, and distinguish this from a debtor's obligation to pay an agent.

Core Idea
A depositary is not ordered to deliver another's property to an agent without the owner's direct instruction.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Book of Agency (Kitab al-Wakalah) regarding trust (amanah). The passage compares two scenarios: a debtor confirming an agent's claim versus a depositary confirming it. Understanding the difference hinges on whose property is at stake and whether the owner has given explicit authorization. By the end, you will be able to apply this distinction to real-life cases of agency and trust.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what action is being ruled upon: the depositary (Khalid) handing over the trust item to a person (Zaid) who claims to be the owner's (Umar's) agent. The ruling is that the depositary is not ordered (لم يؤمر) to do so.
The ruling is a negation of obligation, not a prohibition.
2
Compare with the loan case
The passage contrasts with a previous case where a debtor (Khalid) was ordered to pay the debt to the agent because the debt was Khalid's own liability. Here, the trust item is Umar's property, not Khalid's.
The distinction lies in ownership of the property in the depositary's possession.
3
Identify the underlying principle (asl)
The principle: 'another's property will not be ordered to be handed over despite confirmation of agency.' The owner's explicit authorization is required.
This is a manifestation of the maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (matters are judged by objectives).
4
Trace the qiyas (analogical reasoning)
The asl (original case) is the debt scenario (Khalid's own liability). The far‘ (new case) is the trust scenario (Umar's property). The illa (effective cause) is that in the asl, the property is the depositary's own, while in the far‘ it is another's. Therefore, the ruling differs.
The illa is jaliyyah (clear) extracted by tahqiq al-manat.
5
Apply the ruling to practical scenarios
In modern practice, a trustee or custodian should not release assets to anyone claiming to be the owner's agent without direct verification from the owner, even if third parties confirm the agency.
This protects the owner's property from unauthorized transfer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوكالة
المودع لم يؤمر بالتسليم اليه.
کر دی کہ تم عمر کے وکیل ہو تو خالد کو حکم نہیں دیا جائے گا کہ امانت کی چیز اس کو دیدو۔
امانت کی چیز میں وہی چیز دی جاتی ہے جو امانت رکھی گئی ہے۔ امانت رکھنے والا اپنی طرف سے کوئی چیز نہیں دے سکے گا۔ اس لئے خالد نے تصدیق کردی کہ زید وکیل ہے تو عمر کی امانت شدہ چیز زید کے حوالے کرنے کے لئے نہیں کہا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ عمر نے نہیں کہا ہے کہ زید میرا وکیل ہے۔ وہ اب تک غائب ہے اس لئے امانت کی چیز وکیل کے حوالے کرنے نہیں کہا جائے گا۔ اوپر کے مسئلے میں تو قرض کی رقم خود خالد کی رقم تھی اس لئے دینے کو کہا گیا، یہاں امانت کی رقم عمر کی ہے خالد کی نہیں ہے۔
اصول یہ مسئلہ اس پر ہے کہ دوسرے کی چیز وکالت کی تصدیق کے باوجود حوالے کرنے نہیں کہا جائے گا۔
مودع : امانت پر رکھنے والا آدمی ، ودع سے مشتق ہے۔
Book of Agency. The depositary is not ordered to hand over to him. If (someone) says that you are the agent of Umar, then Khalid will not be ordered to give the trust item to him. In the trust item, only that which was deposited is given. The depositor cannot give anything from his own side. Therefore, if Khalid confirms that Zaid is the agent, then Umar's deposited item will not be ordered to be handed over to Zaid, because Umar did not say that Zaid is his agent. He is still absent, so the trust item will not be ordered to be handed over to the agent. In the previous issue, the loan amount was Khalid's own money, so he was ordered to give it; here the trust amount is Umar's, not Khalid's. The principle is that this issue is based on the fact that another's property will not be ordered to be handed over despite confirmation of agency. Mudi': the person who keeps the trust, derived from wada'a.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Agency addresses a specific ruling concerning trust (amanah). The passage type is a ruling with comparative reasoning. The scenario: Khalid is holding a trust item belonging to Umar. Someone (possibly the agent) says that Zaid is Umar's agent. Khalid confirms this. Despite this confirmation, the depositary (Khalid) is not ordered to hand over the trust item to Zaid, because Umar himself has not authorized Zaid to receive it, and Umar is absent. The author contrasts this with a previous case in which a debtor (Khalid) was ordered to pay the debt to the agent because the debt was Khalid's own liability. Here, the trust item is Umar's property, not Khalid's, so Khalid cannot be forced to deliver it merely based on an agent's claim. The underlying principle (asl) is that another person's property cannot be ordered to be handed over even if agency is confirmed, because the owner's explicit instruction is required. The passage ends with a definition of 'mudi' (depositary/ruler of trust).

Key Terms
مودع (mudi'): The person who keeps a deposit (amanah); the depositary.
وکیل (wakil): A person appointed by another to act on their behalf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is the depositary not ordered to hand over the trust to the agent, even though the agent's status is confirmed?
A) Because the trust item belongs to the depositary.
B) Because the owner (Umar) did not authorize the agent to receive it.
C) Because the agent is not a Muslim.
D) Because the depositary already delivered the item.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a depositary is not obligated to deliver trust property to an agent without the owner's explicit authorization, as the property does not belong to the depositary.

Exception to Acceptance Condition in Suretyship
States an exception where creditor's acceptance is not required

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the specific case where suretyship is valid without creditor's acceptance and explain the Hanafi rationale based on necessity and treatment as a will.

Core Idea
If a terminally ill person asks his heir to be surety, it is valid without creditor's acceptance because it is a will, not a contract.

Suretyship (kafālah) generally requires the creditor's acceptance in the same council. However, the Hanafi school recognizes a unique exception: when a person in terminal illness appoints his heir as surety for his debts, the suretyship is valid even without the creditor's acceptance. In this lesson, we analyze this exception, its rationale, and how it differs from ordinary suretyship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The passage begins by reaffirming the standard requirement: suretyship is conditional upon the creditor's acceptance in the council of contract. Without it, the contract is not binding.
This is the default Hanafi position.
2
Locate the exception
The text explicitly states 'except in one case' ('illa fi mas'alah wahidah) and describes it: a sick person asks his heir to become surety for his debts, and the heir does so in the creditors' absence.
The phrase 'gheebat al-ghuramā' indicates creditors are not present to accept.
3
Understand the rationale
The commentary explains that this is not a true suretyship but a will (wasiyyah) to pay the debts. The heir acts as an executor (wasī). Hence, acceptance is not needed.
The legal reclassification changes the ruling.
4
Note the necessity
The passage cites necessity (darūrah): at the time of death, not all creditors may be present. Without this exception, debts might be lost. The law accommodates this practical need.
Necessity does not create a new rule but recognizes an existing gap.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الا بقبول المكفول له فى مجلس العقد ۱۴۴۷ (۳۰) الا في مسئلة واحدة و هي ان يقول المريض لوارثه تكفل عنى بما على من الدين فتكفل به مع غيبة الغرماء جاز ۱۳۸ (۳۱) واذا كان الدين على اثنين وكل واحد منهما كفيل ضامن عن الآخر فما بھی کفیل بن جائے گا۔ جیسے کہ فضولی کے نکاح کو مجلس کے علاوہ جہاں خبر ملے اور قبول کر لے تب بھی نکاح ہو جاتا ہے۔ اسی طرح مجلس کفالت کے علاوہ میں ممکفول لہ قبول کر لے تب بھی کفالت درست ہو جائے گی۔ [۱۴۴۷] (۳۰) مگر ایک مسئلہ میں وہ یہ کہ بیمار اپنے وارث سے کہے کہ میری جانب سے کفیل ہو جاؤ اس چیز کا جو میرے اوپر دین ہے، پس اس کا کفیل بن گیا قرض خواہوں کی عدم موجودگی میں تو جائز ہے۔ یہ ایک مسئلہ ایسا ہے کہ مغول نہ کفالت کو قبول نہ کرے بلکہ مغول لہ غائب ہو تب بھی کفالت درست ہے وہ یہ ہے کہ ایک آدمی مرض الموت میں مبتلا ہے اور اپنے وارث سے کہتا ہے کہ مجھ پر جتنا دین ہے اس کا تم کفیل بن جاؤ اور وہ مکفول لہ کی عدم موجودگی میں کفیل بن جائے تو اس صورت میں وارث کا کفیل بنا صحیح ہے۔ یہ اصل میں کفیل بنا نہیں ہے بلکہ حقیقت میں قرض خواہوں کو قرض ادا کرنے کے لئے وصیت ہے۔ اور کفیل حقیقت میں وصی ہے اس لئے اس صورت میں مکفول لہ کے قبول کئے بغیر بھی کفیل بننا درست ہے (۲) یہاں مجبوری بھی ہے کیونکہ موت کے وقت تمام قرض خواہ حاضر نہیں ہوتے ہیں۔ اب اگر کفیل یا وسی نہ بنایا جائے تو قرض خواہوں کا قرض ضائع ہو جائے گا۔ اس لئے مکفول لہ کے قبول کئے بغیر کفیل بننا درست ہے۔
Except with the acceptance of the creditor in the council of contract. (30) Except in one case: that a sick person says to his heir: 'Become a surety for me for the debt I owe,' and he becomes surety in the absence of the creditors, it is valid. (31) And if the debt is on two persons and each of them is a surety and guarantor for the other... [Urdu explanation: Just as a marriage by a unauthorized agent becomes valid if the parties accept outside the council, similarly suretyship becomes valid if the creditor accepts outside the council. (30) But one case: a sick person asks his heir to be surety for his debt, and he becomes surety in the absence of creditors, it is valid. This is a case where the creditor does not accept, but it is valid. The explanation: a person in terminal illness asks his heir to be surety for his debts, and the heir becomes surety without the creditor being present; this is valid. In reality, this is not a suretyship but a will to pay the debts, and the surety is actually an executor. Therefore, becoming a surety without the creditor's acceptance is valid. There is a necessity because at the time of death not all creditors are present; if no surety or executor is appointed, the debt would be lost.]
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage discusses an exception to the general rule that suretyship requires the creditor's acceptance in the same council. The general rule is that if the creditor accepts outside the council, the suretyship is still valid, similar to marriage by an unauthorized agent. However, there is one specific case where the creditor's acceptance is not required at all: when a person in terminal illness asks his heir to become surety for his debts, and the heir does so even without the creditors being present. The commentary explains that this is not a true suretyship but rather a will (wasiyyah) to pay the debts. The heir acts as an executor (wasi). This exception is due to necessity, as creditors may not be present at the time of death. The ruling is from the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafālah): Suretyship; a contract where one person guarantees the debt or obligation of another.
مكفول له (makfūl lahu): The creditor or beneficiary of the suretyship.
مرض الموت (marad al-mawt): Terminal illness, a state of sickness that is expected to lead to death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the heir's suretyship valid without creditor's acceptance?
A) Because the heir is obligatied by law to pay the debts
B) Because it is considered a will, not a suretyship contract
C) Because the creditor is automatically deemed to accept
D) Because the debtor's terminal illness cancels all contractual conditions

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi exception for suretyship without creditor's acceptance is limited to heirs of terminally ill debtors and is legally recharacterized as a will.

Two Debtors as Mutual Sureties: Recovery After Half
Ruling on mutual suretyship with proof from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that when two mutual sureties pay a debt, they can only reclaim from the other the amount exceeding their own share.

Core Idea
Own debt (aṣl) is paid first; suretyship (farʿ) only applies after that.

This ruling clarifies a common situation where two people both owe money and are also each other's sureties. The key question: when one pays the debt, how much can he recover from the other? The Hanafi answer is based on the priority of one's own debt over suretyship. Let us examine the ruling, its proof, and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Two debtors owe one debt, and each is a surety for the other. If one pays, the ruling is that he recovers only the amount exceeding his own share. Identify the act (mutual suretyship payment) and the ruling per Hanafi fiqh.
The act is 'payment by a mutual surety'.
2
Locate the dalil
The text cites a hadith from Sunan al-Nasa'i emphasizing that debt must be paid before Paradise is entered, even if one dies a martyr. This shows the weight of one's own debt.
The hadith is about the severity of unpaid debt.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof works)
Since the hadith stresses that one's own debt must be settled, the Hanafi jurists deduce that when a person pays money, it is first attributed to his own debt (aṣl), and only surplus counts as suretyship (farʿ).
The principle: asl before far'.
4
Note the practical consequence
Apply to an example: each owes 500. If one pays 500, that's his own share – nothing to reclaim. If he pays 600, the extra 100 is paid as surety, reclaimable from his partner.
This prevents disputes over how much is shared.

قرض ادا کرنے کے لئے وصیت ہے۔ اور کفیل حقیقت میں وصی ہے اس لئے اس صورت میں مکفول لہ کے قبول کئے بغیر بھی کفیل بننا درست ہے (۲) یہاں مجبوری بھی ہے کیونکہ موت کے وقت تمام قرض خواہ حاضر نہیں ہوتے ہیں۔ اب اگر کفیل یا وسی نہ بنایا جائے تو قرض خواہوں کا قرض ضائع ہو جائے گا۔ اس لئے مکفول لہ کے قبول کئے بغیر کفیل بننا درست ہے۔ ات الغرماء : قرض دینے والے، قرض خواہ ۔ [۱۴۴۸] (۳۱) اگر قرض دو آدمیوں پر ہو اور دونوں میں سے ہر ایک کفیل اور ضامن ہو دوسرے کا تو جو کچھ ان میں سے ایک نے ادا کیا تو وہ شریک سے وصول نہیں کرے گا یہاں تک کہ زیادہ ہو جو ادا کیا آدھے سے، پس وصول کرے گا زیادہ کو۔ دو آدمیوں پر قرض تھا۔ مثلا دو آدمیوں نے ایک غلام ایک ہزار میں خریدا تھا اور دونوں پر آدھی آدھی قیمت قرض تھی یعنی پانچ پانچ سو ور ہم تھے ۔ اور دونوں ایک دوسرے کے کفیل بھی تھے۔ پس ایک نے اگر آدھا قرض یعنی پانچ سو ادا کیا ہے ہونے کی وجہ سے ادا کرنے والے کی جانب سے ادا ہو گا۔ کفالت کے طور پر شریک کی جانب سے ادا نہیں ہو گا۔ اس لئے اس میں سے شریک سے کچھ وصول نہیں کر پائے گا۔ البتہ آدھا سے زیادہ جو کچھ ادا کیا وہ ادا کرنے والے پر قرض نہیں ہے اس لئے ملے ہے کہ وہ کفالت کے طور پر شریک کی جانب سے ادا کیا ہے اس لئے اب اس سے وصول کریگا۔ اصول یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ اصل پہلے ادا ہو گا اور فرع اور مطالبہ بعد میں ادا ہو گا۔ اپنا قرض پہلے ادا ہو اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ قرض ادانہ کرنے پر کافی وعید آئی ہے۔ حدیث میں ہے ۔ عن محمد بن جحش ... فقال والذي نفسي بيده لو ان رجلا قتل في سبيل الله ثم احيى ثم قتل ثم احيي ثم قتل و عليه دين ما دخل الجنة حتى يقضى عنه . عنه دينه (الف) ( نسائی شریف ، باب التغلیظ فی الدین ص ۲۰۲ نمبر ۸ ۴۶۸) اس حدیث کی بنا پر اپنا دین پہلے ادا ہوگا
And if the debt is on two persons and each of them is a surety and guarantor for the other, then what one of them pays, he will not reclaim from his partner until what he pays exceeds half; then he reclaims the excess. [Explanation: For example, two persons bought a slave for one thousand and each owed half, five hundred each. They were mutual sureties. If one pays half (500), that half is counted as his own share, not his partner's. So he cannot reclaim anything from his partner until he pays more than half. The extra above half is paid as a surety for his partner, so he can reclaim that. The principle is that the original debt (asl) has priority over the suretyship (far'). The hadith: The Prophet (SAW) said, 'If a man were killed in the way of Allah, then revived, then killed, then revived, then killed, and he had debt, he would not enter Paradise until his debt is paid.' (Nasai) Based on this, one's own debt is paid first.]
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This ruling deals with two persons who are both debtors (each owing a share) and also mutual sureties for each other. The principle is that when one of them pays, the payment is first applied to his own debt (asl). Only if he pays more than his own share does the excess count as suretyship for his partner, and he can reclaim that excess from the partner. The commentary uses the example of a slave bought for 1000 dirhams, each debtor owing 500. If one pays 500, that is his own debt, he cannot claim from the partner. But if he pays 600, then 500 is his own and 100 is paid as surety for his partner, so he can claim the 100. The proof is a hadith emphasizing the severe warning against leaving debts unpaid, so one's own debt must be settled first before considering suretyship obligations.

Key Terms
أصل (aṣl): The original debt or obligation that one is primarily responsible for.
فرع (farʿ): The suretyship obligation, which is secondary to the original debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 300
B) 100
C) 600
D) 500

Key Takeaway
When mutual sureties pay a joint debt, the payer's own share is settled first; only the excess is reclaimable as surety.

Two Mutual Sureties: Recovery of Half
Ruling on mutual sureties' right of recourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on recovery when two mutual sureties pay the debt and apply the half-recovery principle.

Core Idea
Among mutual sureties, each pays half on his own behalf; the paying surety recovers half from his co-surety.

When two persons become sureties for a debtor and also guarantee each other's suretyship, a special rule governs their financial liability. The Hanafi school holds that if one surety pays the full debt, he can reclaim half from his partner, regardless of the amount. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction
Two persons become sureties for a man for a sum of money, with the condition that each is also a surety for his companion (mutual guarantee).
The condition creates a shared liability.
2
Payment by one surety
One surety pays the entire debt to the creditor. This payment covers both his own portion and his partner's portion.
He pays on behalf of both.
3
Calculate recovery
The paying surety reclaims exactly half of what he paid from his co-surety, because each was liable for half of the debt. The ruling applies whether the debt is small or large.
The co-surety must refund that half.
4
Recognize the underlying principle
Each surety is considered to have guaranteed the other's suretyship, so they are equal partners in the obligation. This prevents unjust enrichment.
The principle ensures fairness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۴۴۹] (۳۲) واذا تكفل اثنان عن رجل بالف على ان كل واحد منهما كفيل عن صاحبه فما اداه احدهما يرجع بنصفه على شريكه قليلا كان او كثيرا [۱۴۵۰] (۳۳) نہیں جائز ہے کفالہ مال کتابت کا چاہے آزاد اس کا کفیل بنے چاہے غلام ۔ مکاتب نے کتابت کے لئے مولی کا قرض اپنے سر لیا۔ اس قرض کا کوئی کفیل بننا چاہے تو کفیل نہیں بن سکتا۔
(32) And if two persons become sureties for a man for a thousand, on condition that each of them is a surety for his companion, then what one of them pays, he reclaims half of it from his partner, be it little or much. (33) It is not permissible to be surety for the debt of kitabah, whether the surety is free or slave. The mukatib has taken the debt of kitabah upon himself. Whoever wants to become surety for this debt cannot become surety.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct rulings. First (32): When two persons both act as sureties for a single principal debtor, and they also make each other mutual sureties (each is surety for the other's suretyship), then if one surety pays any amount to the creditor, he can reclaim half of that amount from his co-surety. This is because both are equal in their suretyship obligation; the payment is considered half from himself and half as surety for his partner. Second (33): It is not valid to be a surety for the debt of kitabah (the money a mukatib slave owes to his master for his freedom). This is because kitabah is a contract between master and slave, and the slave's debt is not like a regular debt that can be guaranteed by a third party.

Key Terms
كفيل (kafil): A surety; a person who guarantees the debt of another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two mutual sureties guarantee a debt of 500, and one surety pays the entire 500, how much does he recover from his partner?
A) 500
B) 250
C) 0
D) 125

Key Takeaway
In mutual suretyship, each surety is liable for half; the paying surety recovers half from the co-surety.

Why Suretyship for Kitabah Debt is Invalid
Prohibition of kafalah for kitabah debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi prohibition of suretyship for kitabah debt and identify the unique nature of the kitabah contract.

Core Idea
Kitabah debt is a personal obligation between master and slave; no third party can act as surety.

Kitabah is a contract of manumission where the slave agrees to pay his master for freedom. This debt is not like a regular financial debt; it is tied to the slave's person and potential. The Hanafi school rules that suretyship for this debt is invalid. This lesson explains the rationale and scope.

Guided Path
1
Define kitabah
Kitabah is a contract where a slave (mukatib) agrees to pay his master a sum in installments for his freedom. The debt is a substitute for the slave's future earnings.
The debt is not a standard loan.
2
State the ruling
It is not permissible (nahyun jā'iz) for anyone to become a surety for the kitabah debt, whether the potential surety is free or slave.
The prohibition is absolute.
3
Understand the rationale
The kitabah debt is tied to the slave's ability to earn. If a third party guarantees it, the slave's obligation would be transferred, but the contract is personal to the mukatib.
Also, suretyship would change the nature of the debt.
4
Note the application
This ruling applies regardless of the status of the person wishing to be surety. It is a unique category of debt that cannot be guaranteed.
Other schools may differ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(32) And if two persons become sureties for a man for a thousand, on condition that each of them is a surety for his companion, then what one of them pays, he reclaims half of it from his partner, be it little or much. (33) It is not permissible to be surety for the debt of kitabah, whether the surety is free or slave. The mukatib has taken the debt of kitabah upon himself. Whoever wants to become surety for this debt cannot become surety.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct rulings. First (32): When two persons both act as sureties for a single principal debtor, and they also make each other mutual sureties (each is surety for the other's suretyship), then if one surety pays any amount to the creditor, he can reclaim half of that amount from his co-surety. This is because both are equal in their suretyship obligation; the payment is considered half from himself and half as surety for his partner. Second (33): It is not valid to be a surety for the debt of kitabah (the money a mukatib slave owes to his master for his freedom). This is because kitabah is a contract between master and slave, and the slave's debt is not like a regular debt that can be guaranteed by a third party.

Key Terms
كتابة (kitābah): A contract of manumission where a slave agrees to pay a sum to the master in installments, becoming free upon full payment.
